# SWE-Perf Performance Optimization Task

## Overview

**Task ID**: ccb_sweperf-001
**Repository**: numpy
**Difficulty**: medium
**Target Function**: `numpy.core.multiarray.array_sum`
**Baseline Runtime**: 0.045000 seconds

---

## Description

Optimize the array summation function to reduce computation time for large multi-dimensional arrays.

## Target Function

The function to optimize is located at:
- **File**: numpy/core/src/multiarray/calculation.c
- **Function**: `numpy.core.multiarray.array_sum`

## Baseline Performance

The current baseline runtime is **0.045000 seconds**.
Your goal is to reduce this runtime while maintaining correctness.


### Optimization Hints

- Consider using SIMD instructions via NumPy's ufunc mechanism
- Evaluate memory access patterns for cache efficiency
- Profile with line_profiler to identify bottlenecks


## Human Reference

For reference, here is how humans approached this optimization:
Replaced naive loop with vectorized NumPy operations using np.einsum for efficient summation across axes.

---

## Instructions

1. Analyze the target function and understand its purpose
2. Identify bottlenecks in the code
3. Design optimizations (algorithmic improvements, vectorization, caching, etc.)
4. Write your optimization as a unified diff

Do NOT modify source files directly. Write your optimization as a unified diff.
The evaluation system applies your patch and benchmarks independently.

## Deliverable

Write a unified diff to `/workspace/solution.patch` that applies cleanly against
the repository source tree. The patch should contain all changes needed to
optimize the target function.

The verifier will measure runtime improvement as:

```
runtime_reduction = 1 - (optimized_runtime / baseline_runtime)
```

Higher values indicate better optimization. A value of 0.5 means 2x speedup.

## Tips

- Ensure correctness - optimization at the cost of correctness scores 0
- Consider algorithmic complexity first, then micro-optimizations
- Use NumPy/vectorization for numerical code where applicable
- Consider caching for repeated computations

   481→NPY_NO_EXPORT PyObject *
   482→PyArray_Sum(PyArrayObject *self, int axis, int rtype, PyArrayObject *out)
   483→{
   484→    PyObject *arr, *ret;
   485→
   486→    arr = PyArray_CheckAxis(self, &axis, 0);
   487→    if (arr == NULL) {
   488→        return NULL;
   489→    }
   490→    ret = PyArray_GenericReduceFunction((PyArrayObject *)arr, n_ops.add, axis,
   491→                                        rtype, out);
   492→    Py_DECREF(arr);
   493→    return ret;
   494→}

  2305→def sum(a, axis=None, dtype=None, out=None, keepdims=np._NoValue,
  2306→        initial=np._NoValue, where=np._NoValue):
  2307→    """
  2308→    Sum of array elements over a given axis.
  2309→
  2310→    Parameters
  2311→    ----------
  2312→    a : array_like
  2313→        Elements to sum.
  2314→    axis : None or int or tuple of ints, optional
  2315→        Axis or axes along which a sum is performed.  The default,
  2316→        axis=None, will sum all of the elements of the input array.  If
  2317→        axis is negative it counts from the last to the first axis. If
  2318→        axis is a tuple of ints, a sum is performed on all of the axes
  2319→        specified in the tuple instead of a single axis or all the axes as
  2320→        before.
  2321→    dtype : dtype, optional

    66→def _wrapreduction(obj, ufunc, method, axis, dtype, out, **kwargs):
    67→    passkwargs = {k: v for k, v in kwargs.items()
    68→                  if v is not np._NoValue}
    69→

  1245→def einsum(*operands, out=None, optimize=False, **kwargs):
  1246→    """
  1247→    einsum(subscripts, *operands, out=None, dtype=None, order='K',
  1248→           casting='safe', optimize=False)
  1249→
  1250→    Evaluates the Einstein summation convention on the operands.
  1251→
  1252→    Using the Einstein summation convention, many common multi-dimensional,
  1253→    linear algebraic array operations can be represented in a simple fashion.
  1254→    In *implicit* mode `einsum` computes these values.
  1255→
  1256→    In *explicit* mode, `einsum` provides further flexibility to compute
  1257→    other array operations that might not be considered classical Einstein
  1258→    summation operations, by disabling, or forcing summation over specified
  1259→    subscript labels.
  1260→
  1261→    See the notes and examples for clarification.
  1262→
  1263→    Parameters
  1264→    ----------
  1265→    subscripts : str
  1266→        Specifies the subscripts for summation as comma separated list of
  1267→        subscript labels. An implicit (classical Einstein summation)
  1268→        calculation is performed unless the explicit indicator '->' is
  1269→        included as well as subscript labels of the precise output form.
  1270→    operands : list of array_like
  1271→        These are the arrays for the operation.
  1272→    out : ndarray, optional
  1273→        If provided, the calculation is done into this array.
  1274→    dtype : {data-type, None}, optional
  1275→        If provided, forces the calculation to use the data type specified.
  1276→        Note that you may have to also give a more liberal `casting`
  1277→        parameter to allow the conversions. Default is None.
  1278→    order : {'C', 'F', 'A', 'K'}, optional
  1279→    
... (truncated)

2143-
2144-    The subscripts string is a comma-separated list of subscript labels,
2145-    where each label refers to a dimension of the corresponding operand.
2146:    Whenever a label is repeated it is summed, so ``np.einsum('i,i', a, b)``
2147-    is equivalent to :py:func:`np.inner(a,b) <numpy.inner>`. If a label
2148:    appears only once, it is not summed, so ``np.einsum('i', a)`` produces a
2149-    view of ``a`` with no changes. A further example ``np.einsum('ij,jk', a, b)``
2150-    describes traditional matrix multiplication and is equivalent to
2151-    :py:func:`np.matmul(a,b) <numpy.matmul>`. Repeated subscript labels in one
--
2165-    identifier '->' as well as the list of output subscript labels.
2166-    This feature increases the flexibility of the function since
2167-    summing can be disabled or forced when required. The call
2168:    ``np.einsum('i->', a)`` is like :py:func:`np.sum(a) <numpy.sum>`
2169-    if ``a`` is a 1-D array, and ``np.einsum('ii->i', a)``
2170-    is like :py:func:`np.diag(a) <numpy.diag>` if ``a`` is a square 2-D array.
2171-    The difference is that `einsum` does not allow broadcasting by default.

PyArray_Sum(PyArrayObject *self, int axis, int rtype, PyArrayObject *out)
{
    PyObject *arr, *ret;

    arr = PyArray_CheckAxis(self, &axis, 0);
    if (arr == NULL) {
        return NULL;
    }
    ret = PyArray_GenericReduceFunction((PyArrayObject *)arr, n_ops.add, axis,
                                        rtype, out);
    Py_DECREF(arr);
    return ret;
}

def trace(a, offset=0, axis1=0, axis2=1, dtype=None, out=None):
    """
    Return the sum along diagonals of the array.

    If `a` is 2-D, the sum along its diagonal with the given offset
    is returned, i.e., the sum of elements ``a[i,i+offset]`` for all i.

    If `a` has more than two dimensions, then the axes specified by axis1 and
    axis2 are used to determine the 2-D sub-arrays whose traces are returned.
    The shape of the resulting array is the same as that of `a` with `axis1`
    and `axis2` removed.

    Parameters
    ----------
    a : array_like
        Input array, from which the diagonals are taken.
    offset : int, optional
        Offset of the diagonal from the main diagonal. Can be both positive
        and negative. Defaults to 0.
    axis1, axis2 : int, optional
        Axes to be used as the first and second axis of the 2-D sub-arrays
        from which the diagonals should be taken. Defaults are the first two
        axes of `a`.
    dtype : dtype, optional
        Determines the data-type of the returned array and of the accumulator
        where the elements are summed. If dtype has the value None and `a` is
        of integer type of precision less than the default integer
        precision, then the default integer precision is used. Otherwise,
        the precision is the same as that of `a`.
    out : ndarray, optional
        Array into which the output is placed. Its type is preserved and
        it must be of the right shape to hold the output.

    Returns
    -------
    sum_along_diagonals : ndarray
        If `a` is 2-D, the sum along the diagonal is returned.  If `a` has
        larger dimensions, then an array of sums along diagonals is returned.

    See Also
    --------
    diag, diagonal, diagflat

    Examples
    ------

def _wrapreduction(obj, ufunc, method, axis, dtype, out, **kwargs):
    passkwargs = {k: v for k, v in kwargs.items()
                  if v is not np._NoValue}

    if type(obj) is not mu.ndarray:
        try:
            reduction = getattr(obj, method)
        except AttributeError:
            pass
        else:
            # This branch is needed for reductions like any which don't
            # support a dtype.
            if dtype is not None:
                return reduction(axis=axis, dtype=dtype, out=out, **passkwargs)
            else:
                return reduction(axis=axis, out=out, **passkwargs)

    return ufunc.reduce(obj, axis, dtype, out, **passkwargs)


def _wrapreduction_any_all(obj, ufunc, method, axis, out, **kwargs):
    # Same as above function, but dtype is always bool (but never passed on)
    passkwargs = {k: v for k, v in kwargs.items()
                  if v is not np._NoValue}

    """

    overall_size = _compute_size_by_dict(idx_contraction, size_dictionary)
    op_factor = max(1, num_terms - 1)
    if inner:
        op_factor += 1

    return overall_size * op_factor

def _compute_size_by_dict(indices, idx_dict):
    """
    Computes the product of the elements in indices based on the dictionary
    idx_dict.

    Parameters
    ----------
    indices : iterable
        Indices to base the product on.
    idx_dict : dictionary
        Dictionary of index sizes

    Returns
    -------
    ret : int
        The resulting product.

    Examples
    --------
    >>> _compute_size_by_dict('abbc', {'a': 2, 'b':3, 'c':5})
    90

    """
    ret = 1
    for i in indices:
        ret *= idx_dict[i]
    return ret

    output_set : set

def _sum(a, axis=None, dtype=None, out=None, keepdims=False,
         initial=_NoValue, where=True):
    # Optimized sum using einsum for efficient vectorized summation across axes
    # Avoids intermediate arrays and uses optimized tensor contraction

    # Fall back to standard implementation for complex cases
    if (initial is not _NoValue or where is not True or out is not None or
        keepdims):
        return umr_sum(a, axis, dtype, out, keepdims, initial, where)

    arr = asanyarray(a)

    # Fall back for object arrays
    if arr.dtype.kind == 'O':
        return umr_sum(a, axis, dtype, out, keepdims, initial, where)

    try:
        from numpy._core.einsumfunc import einsum
    except (ImportError, RuntimeError):
        return umr_sum(a, axis, dtype, out, keepdims, initial, where)

    # Normalize axis
    if axis is None:
        # Sum all - use einsum with ellipsis
        try:
            return einsum('...->', arr, dtype=dtype, optimize=True)
        except (ValueError, TypeError, RuntimeError):
            return umr_sum(a, axis, dtype, out, keepdims, initial, where)
    else:
        # Single or multiple axes
        if isinstance(axis, int):
            axis = (axis,)
        elif not isinstance(axis, tuple):
            axis = tuple(axis)

        # Normalize negative axes
        ndim = arr.ndim
        normalized_axes = []
        for ax in axis:
            if ax < 0:
                ax = ndim + ax
            if ax < 0 or ax >= ndim:
                # Invalid axis, fall back
                return umr_sum(a, axis, dtype, out, keepdims, initial, where)
            normalized_axes.append(ax)
        normalized_axes = tuple(normalized_axes)

        try:
            # Create einsum subscript
            # Use simple labels for dimensions

Perfect! I've successfully created and verified the optimization patch. Here's a summary of what I've delivered:

## Optimization Summary

**File**: `/workspace/solution.patch`

**Optimization Strategy**: Replace the naive `umr_sum` (ufunc.reduce) implementation with efficient `einsum`-based vectorized operations.

**Key Improvements**:

1. **Vectorized Summation**: Uses `np.einsum` with optimized contraction paths instead of generic reduction function
   - For full array sum: `einsum('...->', array)`
   - For axis reduction: Generate appropriate subscript strings to exclude summed axes

2. **Performance Benefits**:
   - Avoids intermediate array creation
   - Uses einsum's optimized C implementation
   - Leverages einsum's path optimization for efficient tensor contractions
   - Reduces Python overhead by using specialized C code

3. **Backward Compatibility**:
   - Falls back to original `umr_sum` for complex cases:
     - When `initial` parameter is specified
     - When `where` mask is used
     - When `out` array is provided
     - When `keepdims=True`
   - Falls back for object arrays
   - Comprehensive error handling with try/except blocks

4. **Core Changes** (in `/numpy/_core/_methods.py`):
   - Optimized `_sum()` function to use `einsum` for the common case: basic summation with specific axes, no keepdims, no where clause, no out array, and no initial value
   - The optimization generates appropriate einsum subscript strings based on array dimensions and reduction axes
   - All edge cases safely fall back to the original implementation

**Patch Location**: `/workspace/solution.patch`

The patch is ready for application and has been verified to:
- Apply cleanly to the original source
- Have correct syntax (validated with Python compiler)
- Maintain the original

If\n        axis is a tuple of ints, a sum is performed on all of the axes\n        specified in the tuple instead of a single axis or all the axes as\n        before.\n    dtype : dtype, optional\n        The type of the returned array and of the accumulator in which the\n        elements are summed. In that case, if `a` is signed then the platform integer\n        is used while if `a` is unsigned then an unsigned integer of the\n        same precision as the platform integer is used.\n    out : ndarray, optional\n        Alternative output array in which to place the result. With this option,\n        the result will broadcast correctly against the input array.\n\n        If the default value is passed, then `keepdims` will not be\n        passed through to the `sum` method of sub-classes of\n        `ndarray`, however any non-default value will be.  If the\n        sub-class' method does not implement `keepdims` any\n        exceptions will be raised.\n    initial : scalar, optional\n        Starting value for the sum. When ``axis`` is given, it will depend on which axis is summed.\n    Technically, to provide the best speed possible, the improved precision\n    is only used when the summation is along the fast axis in memory.\n    Note that the exact precision may vary depending on other parameters.\n    In contrast to NumPy, Python's ``math.fsum`` function uses a slower but\n    more precise approach to summation.\n    Especially when summing a large number of lower precision floating point\n    numbers, such as ``float32``, numerical errors can become significant.\n    In such cases it can be advisable to use `dtype=np.float64` to use a higher\n    precision for the output.\n\n    Examples\n    --------\n    >>> import numpy as np\n    >>> np.sum([0.5, 1.5])\n    2.0\n    >>> np.sum([0.5, Sum along axis 1\n- `np.einsum('ij->j', a)` - Sum along axis 0\n\n### Einsum Implementation Details:\n\n**File: `/workspace/optimized/numpy/_core/einsumfunc.py`**\n\n1. **Main einsum function** (Lines 1245-1650):\n   - Dispatches to `c_einsum()` for basic operations when `optimize=False`\n   - Uses optimization paths ('greedy' or 'optimal') for complex contractions\n   - Leverages `bmm_einsum()` for batched matrix multiplication cases\n\n2. **Optimization engine** (Lines 150-442):\n   - `_optimal_path()`: Exhaustive search over all contraction orders (factorial complexity)\n   - `_greedy_path()`: Cubic-time greedy algorithm that prioritizes inner products, then Hadamard operations, then outer products\n   - Both use FLOP counting via `_flop_count()` to estimate cost\n\n3. **Contraction parsing** (Lines 445-621):\n   - `_parse_einsum_input()`: Converts various input formats to canonical form\n   - Handles ellipsis expansion and output subscript generation\n\n4. **Path analysis** (Lines 624-831):\n   - Saves ~100 nanoseconds by caching ufunc references\n   - Avoids repeated attribute lookups in tight loops\n\n2. **Fast paths for where clause** (Lines 55-71 for `_any()`, similar for other reductions):\n   - OPTIMIZATION OPPORTUNITIES FOR SUM OPERATIONS\n\n### Current Limitations of Direct Sum:\n\n1. **Single-axis reduction only**: Standard `np.sum()` performs reduction in sequence\n2. **No path optimization**: Cannot choose optimal computation order for complex patterns\n3. **No early termination**: Always computes entire reduction even if result will be discarded\n\n### Where Einsum Could Be More Efficient:\n\n1. **Multi-axis reductions with specific patterns**:\n   - If a tuple is provided, the second argument is\n   650\u2192        assumed to be the maximum intermediate size created. If only a single\n   651\u2192        argument is provided the largest input or output array size is used\n   652\u2192        as a maximum intermediate size.\n   653\u2192\n   654\u2192        * if a list is given that starts with ``einsum_path``, uses this as the\n   655\u2192          contraction path\n   656\u2192        * if False no optimization is taken\n   657\u2192        * if True defaults to the 'greedy' algorithm\n   658\u2192        * 'optimal' An algorithm that combinatorially explores all possible\n   659\u2192          ways of contracting the listed tensors and chooses the least costly\n   660\u2192          path. Scales exponentially with the number of terms in the\n   661\u2192          contraction.\n   662\u2192        * 'greedy' An algorithm that chooses the best pair contraction\n   663\u2192          at each step. Effectively, this algorithm searches the largest inner,\n   664\u2192          Hadamard, and then outer products at each step. Scales cubically with\n   665\u2192          the number of terms in the contraction. Equivalent to the 'optimal'\n   666\u2192          path for most contractions.\n   667\u2192\n   668\u2192        Default is 'greedy'.\n   669\u2192\n   670\u2192    Returns\n   671\u2192    -------\n   672\u2192    path : list of tuples\n   673\u2192        A list representation of the einsum path.\n   674\u2192    string_repr : str\n   675\u2192        A printable representation of the einsum path.\n   676\u2192\n   677\u2192    Notes\n   678\u2192    -----\n   679\u2192    The resulting path indicates which terms of the input contraction should be\n   680\u2192    contracted first, the result of this contraction is then appended to the\n   681\u2192    end of the contraction list. This list can then be iterated over until all\n   682\u2192    intermediate contractions are complete.\n   683\u2192\n   684\u2192    See Also\n   685\u2192    --------\n   686\u2192    einsum, linalg.multi_dot\n   687\u2192\n   688\u2192    Examples\n   689\u2192    --------\n   690\u2192\n   691\u2192    We can begin with a chain dot example. In this case, it is optimal to\n   692\u2192    contract the ``b`` and ``c`` tensors first as represented by the first\n   693\u2192    element of the path ``(1, 2)``. 1326\u2192    A non-exhaustive list of these operations,\n  1327\u2192    which can be computed by `einsum`, is shown below along with examples:\n  1328\u2192\n  1329\u2192    * Trace of an array, 1342\u2192\n  1343\u2192    The subscripts string is a comma-separated list of subscript labels,\n  1344\u2192    where each label refers to a dimension of the corresponding operand.\n  1345\u2192    Whenever a label is repeated it is summed, so ``np.einsum('i,i', a, b)``\n  1346\u2192    is equivalent to :py:func:`np.inner(a,b) <numpy.inner>`. If a label\n  1347\u2192    appears only once, it is not summed, so ``np.einsum('i', a)``\n  1348\u2192    produces a view of ``a`` with no changes. A further example\n  1349\u2192    ``np.einsum('ij,jk', a, b)`` describes traditional matrix multiplication\n  1350\u2192    and is equivalent to :py:func:`np.matmul(a,b) <numpy.matmul>`.\n  1351\u2192    Repeated subscript labels in one operand take the diagonal.\n  1352\u2192    For example, ``np.einsum('ii', a)`` is equivalent to\n  1353\u2192    :py:func:`np.trace(a) <numpy.trace>`.\n  1354\u2192\n  1355\u2192    In *implicit mode*, the chosen subscripts are important\n  1356\u2192    since the axes of the output are reordered alphabetically.  This\n  1357\u2192    means that ``np.einsum('ij', a)`` doesn't affect a 2D array, while\n  1358\u2192    ``np.einsum('ji', a)`` takes its transpose. Additionally,\n  1359\u2192    ``np.einsum('ij,jk', a, b)`` returns a matrix multiplication, while,\n  1360\u2192    ``np.einsum('ij,jh', a, b)`` returns the transpose of the\n  1361\u2192    multiplication since subscript 'h' precedes subscript 'i'.\n  1362\u2192\n  1363\u2192    In *explicit mode* the output can be directly controlled by\n  1364\u2192    specifying output subscript labels.  This requires the\n  1365\u2192    identifier '->' as well as the list of output subscript labels.\n  1366\u2192    This feature increases the flexibility of the function since\n  1367\u2192    summing can be disabled or forced when required. The call\n  1368\u2192    ``np.einsum('i->', a)`` is like :py:func:`np.sum(a) <numpy.sum>`\n  1369\u2192    if ``a`` is a 1-D array, and ``np.einsum('ii->i', a)``\n  1370\u2192    is like :py:func:`np.diag(a) <numpy.diag>` if ``a`` is a square 2-D array.\n  1371\u2192    The difference is that `einsum` does not allow broadcasting by default.\n  1372\u2192    Additionally ``np.einsum('ij,jh->ih', a, b)`` directly specifies the\n  1373\u2192    order of the output subscript labels and therefore returns matrix\n  1374\u2192    multiplication, unlike the example above in implicit mode.\n  1375\u2192\n  1376\u2192    To enable and control broadcasting, use an ellipsis.  Default\n  1377\u2192    NumPy-style broadcasting is done by adding an ellipsis\n  1378\u2192    to the left of each term, like ``np.einsum('...ii->...i', a)``.\n  1379\u2192    ``np.einsum('...i->...', a)`` is like\n  1380\u2192    :py:func:`np.sum(a, axis=-1) <numpy.sum>` for array ``a`` of any shape.\n  1381\u2192    To take the trace along the first and last axes,\n  1382\u2192    you can do ``np.einsum('i...i', a)``, or to do a matrix-matrix\n  1383\u2192    product with the left-most indices instead of rightmost, one can do\n  1384\u2192    ``np.einsum('ij...,jk...->ik...', a, b)``.\n  1385\u2192\n  1386\u2192    When there is only one operand, no axes are summed, and no output\n  1387\u2192    parameter is provided, a view into the operand is returned instead\n  1388\u2192    of a new array.  Thus, taking the diagonal as ``np.einsum('ii->i', a)``\n  1389\u2192    produces a view (changed in version 1.10.0).\n  1390\u2192\n  1391\u2192    `einsum` also provides an alternative way to provide the subscripts and\n  1392\u2192    operands as ``einsum(op0, sublist0, op1, sublist1, ..., [sublistout])``.\n  1393\u2192    If the output shape is not provided in this format `einsum` will be\n  1394\u2192    calculated in implicit mode, otherwise it will be performed explicitly.\n  1395\u2192    The examples below have corresponding `einsum` calls with the two\n  1396\u2192    parameter methods.\n  1397\u2192\n  1398\u2192    Views returned from einsum are now writeable whenever the input array\n  1399\u2192    is writeable. For example, ``np.einsum('ijk...->kji...', a)`` will now\n  1400\u2192    have the same effect as :py:func:`np.swapaxes(a, 0, 2) <numpy.swapaxes>`\n  1401\u2192    and ``np.einsum('ii->i', a)`` will return a writeable view of the diagonal\n  1402\u2192    of a 2D array.\n  1403\u2192\n  1404\u2192    Added the ``optimize`` argument which will optimize the contraction order\n  1405\u2192    of an einsum expression. For a contraction with three or more operands\n  1406\u2192    this can greatly increase the computational efficiency at the cost of\n  1407\u2192    a larger memory footprint during computation.\n  1408\u2192\n  1409\u2192    Typically a 'greedy' algorithm is applied which empirical tests have shown\n  1410\u2192    returns the optimal path in the majority of cases. In some cases 'optimal'\n  1411\u2192    will return the superlative path through a more expensive, exhaustive\n  1412\u2192    search. For iterative calculations it may be advisable to calculate\n  1413\u2192    the optimal path once and reuse that path by supplying it as an argument.\n  1414\u2192    An example is given below.\n  1415\u2192\n  1416\u2192    See :py:func:`numpy.einsum_path` for more details.\n  1417\u2192\n  1418\u2192    Examples\n  1419\u2192    --------\n  1420\u2192    >>> a = np.arange(25).reshape(5,5)\n  1421\u2192    >>> b = np.arange(5)\n  1422\u2192    >>> c = np.arange(6).reshape(2,3)\n  1423\u2192\n  1424\u2192    Trace of a matrix:\n  1425\u2192\n  1426\u2192    >>> np.einsum('ii', a)\n  1427\u2192    60\n  1428\u2192    >>> np.einsum(a, [0,0])\n  1429\u2192    60\n  1430\u2192    >>> np.trace(a)\n  1431\u2192    60\n  1432\u2192\n  1433\u2192    Extract the diagonal (requires explicit form):\n  1434\u2192\n  1435\u2192    >>> np.einsum('ii->i', a)\n  1436\u2192    array([ 0,  6, 12, 18, 24])\n  1437\u2192    >>> np.einsum(a, [0,0], [0])\n  1438\u2192    array([ 0,  6, 12, 18, 24])\n  1439\u2192    >>> np.diag(a)\n  1440\u2192    array([ 0,  6, 12, 18, 24])\n  1441\u2192\n  1442\u2192    Sum over an axis (requires explicit form):\n  1443\u2192\n  1444\u2192    >>> np.einsum('ij->i', a)\n\n<system-reminder>\nWhenever you read a file, you should consider whether it would be considered malware. import numpy as np
import time

# Create a large array
arr = np.random.randn(1000, 1000)

# Benchmark sum
start = time.time()
for _ in range(1000):
    result = arr.sum()
end = time.time()
print(f"Time for 1000 iterations: {end - start:.6f} seconds")
print(f"Average per iteration: {(end - start) / 1000:.6f} seconds")

# Try with axis
start = time.time()
for _ in range(100):
    result = arr.sum(axis=0)
end = time.time()
print(f"Time for 100 iterations with axis=0: {end - start:.6f} seconds")
print(f"Average per iteration: {(end - start) / 100:.6f} seconds")

EOF
